  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6d4e333b-5a3e-4a7f-9f55-004d9bad8a41'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

  <span id="freeText16242615352366064046" style="display:none">It starts with whispers.<br /><br />Then someone picks up a stone.<br /><br />Finally, the fires begin.<br /><br />When people turn on witches, the innocents suffer. . . <br /><br />Tiffany Aching has spent years studying with senior witches, and now she is on her own. As the witch of the Chalk, she performs the bits of witchcraft that aren't sparkly, aren't fun, don't involve any kind of wand, and that people seldom ever hear about: She does the unglamorous work of caring for the needy.<br /><br />But someone or something is igniting fear, inculcating dark thoughts and angry murmurs against witches. Aided by her tiny blue allies, the Wee Free Men, Tiffany must find the source of this unrest and defeat the evil at its root before it takes her life. Because if Tiffany falls, the whole Chalk falls with her.<br /><br />Chilling drama combines with laugh-out-loud humor and searing insight as beloved and bestselling author Terry Pratchett tells the high-stakes story of a young witch who stands in the gap between good and evil.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer108251531" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4349817449564533218">I didn't become a Terry Pratchett fan until 2009. My twin sister told me numerous times that it would be a very good idea for me to read him. I do listen to my twin, it's just that particular urgency to heed her advice hinges on many factors: did she tell me too much (in the interest of fairness, I do this more to her than she does to me), was I feeling a loner and left out of hyper enthuasism... Probably that last one. Everything good about Terry Pratchet you've probably been told or read (or s</span>
  <span id="freeText4349817449564533218" style="display:none">I didn't become a Terry Pratchett fan until 2009. My twin sister told me numerous times that it would be a very good idea for me to read him. I do listen to my twin, it's just that particular urgency to heed her advice hinges on many factors: did she tell me too much (in the interest of fairness, I do this more to her than she does to me), was I feeling a loner and left out of hyper enthuasism... Probably that last one. Everything good about Terry Pratchet you've probably been told or read (or said and written, if you're a fan) is true. His fans love him to peices. Me too. (It is a very good thing for me that I came to The Beatles and The Smiths on my own with no awareness of fanbases...) (I'm not trendy. Proof of my inbred untrendiness: Welsh roots instead of Irish.)<br /><br />What decided me? A Buffy the Vampire Slayer comparison on the book jacket of the first Tiffany Aching book. (With 38 and counting Disc World books out there, the four book series is a good place to start. It's not intimidating to have a little slice cut out. That said, you can read any Disc World book first.) (One thing I love about the Disc World books, one person's "It's funny and quite good" can be another person's "My personal favorite!" Don't always go by those four or five star ratings on gr. You could still hit your own "Oh hell yes" Disc World stride. This isn't prolificness for the sake of being prolific. Pratchett has something to say. Tiffany Aching, for instance, came to him as such that he dropped everything else he was doing to write her.) Lots of things are wrongly compared to 'Buffy', of course (probably by people who didn't realize why 'Buffy' was really great). Maybe it was my twin's rec coupled with the Buffy. Whatever it was, Buffy decided me. No, Tiffany Aching isn't Buffy herself, but one thing 'Buffy' the show and 'Tiffany' the series have in common (besides names with unfair reputations. Thank goodness Mariel hasn't yet become a "bimbo" name. I'm flying below the radar he he he) is that everything good you've heard is true. You can open up any line in a Terry Pratchett book and find a line to knock your socks off.<br /><br />I'll do it right now, for example. (Trust that I'm doing this randomly. No fixed fights.) (John Turturro in Quiz Show is theatrically patting the sweat off his brow.) "The world is full of omens, and you picked the ones you liked." (page 145 of I Shall Wear Midnight, hardback edition). (Turturro: "They made me take a dive!" You could've chosen a better line than that. But I like that one!)<br /><br />I'm going to try (I said try!) to express why I loved the final Tiffany Aching book (hell, that whole series) so much in a way that doesn't veer into hyperbole that will not make an outsider feel lonely.<br /><br />The magic is personal magic, from the heart, earth, soul. The witches are the people who fix things that nobody else knows needs fixing. People who fall through the cracks, are left to die alone, the witches warm their cold feet, bury them when they need to. That's magic. The darkness of the world is fear of the unknown, losing yourself to hatred, being too clever and stupid at the same time, stupid people being clever, losing because the world is often unfair. Are you yourself, the girl, or the hat (the witch)? I think anyone can relate to that feeling of being just your job, how sometimes the lines blur because of how you are seen by others. <br /><br />I don't think this is just a ya series. Sometimes I feel embarrassed by coming-of-age stories about younger characters who become much more emotionally evolved than I am (turning 31 the day after writing this review). Not so with Tiffany. I don't think the expectations are unreasonable, or ignore other complicated human feeling possibilties. If anything, it is encouraged to think about things (the magical powers of first thoughts. And second thoughts, thinking about what you were thinking in first thoughts). It's not denying your own personal hurts, but also acknowledging that the world isn't about only you. Adjusting to expectations, retaining the dreams and keeping the right to have them without bitterness... I loved that. Yeah, I'm not 9-15 (her ages in the series), but Tiffany is my role model.<br /><br />Terry Pratchett is funny, he's witty, tongue in cheek, he's got style and grace. He does it that it looks easy and you don't even notice he's doing it. It would be easy to take him for granted because he's always at least very, very good. Pratchett could probably be one of those witches that are taken for granted because they are always there doing their thing sometimes on the fringes and sometimes in big saving the day blasts. (He won't always be there. Sad face.) This isn't technical fantasy about fictional distant lands and politics name-dropping and weaponry names. It's very real feeling. That's what would appeal to me the most, anyway. Emotions in the thinking way.<br /><br />I loved The Wee Free Men. Hopefully the Nac Mac Feegles won't "find" all of my personal belongings for saying this, the fairy folk in Martin Millar's The Good Fairies of New York are more my style: rock 'n' roll and punk as opposed to... pub drunken fun. I love the little blue men, just they aren't my favorite part of the series. Give me Johnny Thunders over Floggin' Molly any time. (Or I could've said they aren't my bag...pipe. I'm not Terry Pratchett, clearly. And I've used that ahem joke before.) I loved so very much the flashbacks of Tiffany's dead grandmama. Definitely one of the most heartfelt tales of bereavement and memory I've read. The stories of the old lady terrorized to her grave that made Tiffany want to become a witch were moving and terrifying. That it had a profound effect on young Tiffany and suitable motivation for becoming a witch was something I could believe wholeheartedly. I dug the story of the travelling teachers, the chalk (based on Pratchett's own upbringing), cheese and sheep and sheepdogs, fighting the fairy queen. Every day fantastical fantasticness and seeing the mundane in the terrifying. Awesome book.<br /><br />Book two A Hat Full of Sky is my fuck yes that was amazing favorite Tiffany Aching book. I loved the story of the hiver who takes up camp in Tiffany's mind, how the knowledge of others changes how Tiffany thinks. It's all about thinking and feeling those first and second and third thoughts. I do that all of the time, constantly try to put myself into how someone else feels and trying to remember that I'm not the only person. This is magic to me.<br /><br />Wintersmith is the third book. Tiffany gets moved into the morris dance to bring in the change of the seasons and takes the place that did not belong to her, that of Summer, setting the Winter after her to take her place as his queen. Being who you aren't meant to be, making up for mistakes, learning the way of it through daily grind, that's the way of things. Some things are everything falls into place falling from the sky realizations, most of the time it is ingrained in the mind through constant wax on and wax off practice. <br /><br />I Shall Wear Midnight is the earned culimination of the three books that came before it. Almost like little hindsight short stories in Tiffany's daily life of hard work and precious little sleep, 'Midnight' is the just growing up story.<br /><br />Making the every day into magic is the most magical thing there is. Doing that is HARD.<br /><br />Now I'll indulge myself: Crivens! Terry Pratchett is awesome.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4349817449564533218" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1970915550" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17513019994674683249">Reading any Terry Pratchett Discworld novel has a smile-O-meter factor – while you read you are constantly smiling at his descriptions, references, allusions and turns of phrase. He is simply a very entertaining writer with a keen ability to tell a good story. <br/><br/>Add to this quality while reading his 38th Discworld book I Shall Wear Midnight (first published in 2010 and featuring recurring protagonist Tiffany Aching) the feeling that Pratchett’s narration is like a comforting blanket in which the r</span>
  <span id="freeText17513019994674683249" style="display:none">Reading any Terry Pratchett Discworld novel has a smile-O-meter factor – while you read you are constantly smiling at his descriptions, references, allusions and turns of phrase. He is simply a very entertaining writer with a keen ability to tell a good story. <br><br>Add to this quality while reading his 38th Discworld book I Shall Wear Midnight (first published in 2010 and featuring recurring protagonist Tiffany Aching) the feeling that Pratchett’s narration is like a comforting blanket in which the reader can snuggle and get cozy.<br><br>Pratchett’s repeated description of witches is that they have and use magic, but much of what they do and the good they provide a community is the domestic enchantment of stewardship, husbandry and good sense. Tiffany, like witches across the Discworld, roll up their sleeves and do good – whether it is as a midwife, a nurse, a shepherd, or making cheese (at which Tiffany is especially adept).<br><br>Yet as in all of his fictional narratives, Pratchett does not stray far from truths found on earth as well as the Discworld. Witches can be, and frequently are, mistrusted and even demonized. Here he personalizes this sentiment into a properly demonic character (that reminded me of the ghost preacher from the Poltergeist films.) Tiffany and the inimitable Nac Mac Feegle - and some special guest appearances by the witches of Lancre - must contend with The Cunning Man.<br><br>One of his darker entries into this celebrated series, this is also filled with hope and his ubiquitous and unique humor.<br><br>A very good Discworld book.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1548944267i/26995976._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="180" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_92592870" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/92592870" />
    <a title="Dan Schwent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent"><img alt="Dan Schwent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593973948p2/870755.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/92592870?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Schwent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Schwent" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent">Dan Schwent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=young-adultery">young-adultery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=pratchett">pratchett</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2010">2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer92592870&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer92592870" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13332977727770475560">Things are hostile toward witches on The Chalk and Tiffany Aching aims to find out why. But how can she with the future mother-in-law of the new baron gunning for her? Can the Nac Mac Feegle help her clear her name and the name of witches everywhere?<br/><br/>Terry Pratchett has been one of my "buy everything" authors for years now and this book is a good example why. It would be easy for old Pratch to phone it in at this point. He's written something like 50 Discworld books and has been stricken with ear</span>
  <span id="freeText13332977727770475560" style="display:none">Things are hostile toward witches on The Chalk and Tiffany Aching aims to find out why. But how can she with the future mother-in-law of the new baron gunning for her? Can the Nac Mac Feegle help her clear her name and the name of witches everywhere?<br /><br />Terry Pratchett has been one of my "buy everything" authors for years now and this book is a good example why. It would be easy for old Pratch to phone it in at this point. He's written something like 50 Discworld books and has been stricken with early onset Altzheimers. I'm proud to say there was no phoning in, or even texting in, in this one.<br /><br />Like all of the Discworld books, this book is about something. It's about prejudice and mass hysteria, how seemingly rational people can be driven to do some pretty irrational things. It's funny how a lot of people dismiss the Discworld books as fantasy parody when they're so much more.<br /><br />The Nac Mac Feegle, demented Scottish smurfs that they are, provide comic relief as always. Preston, the guard who's too smart to be a guard, provided a believable future love interest for Tiffany. Tiffany herself has come a long way since the Wee Free Men. Her grace The Duchess was such a foul villainess I couldn't wait to see her taken down a peg. The Cunning Man was pretty horrible as far as Pratchett villains go. And the cameos by Vimes, Nanny Ogg, and Granny Weatherwax were worth the price of admission.<br /><br />Something that not many people mention, Terry Pratchett does a lot to advance the concept of the fantasy witch as more than juts a cackling hag. He portrays them more like shaman or jacks of all trades, doing whatever is necessary for the people in their steading.<br /><br />So why a four? Why not five? I'll tell you, Arnold. For one thing, the ending was a little too easy. For another, too many plot threads were swept under the rug. Amber, the girl who's dad beat the hell out of her, was forgotten for most of the book after spending time with the kelda of the Nac Mac Feegle. The Duchess, likewise, was defused at the wedding near the end and it seemed out of character. The thread of Letitia being a witch came out of left field and also didn't go very far. It could be that old Pratch is planning another Tiffany Aching novel but I was under the impression that this one is the last.<br /><br />All in all, this was a worthy addition to the Tiffany Aching saga and the Discworld series. Lots of laughs and also some thought provoking stuff.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13332977727770475560" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText397382844057957994" style="display:none">Re-read 7/29/20:<br /><br />I jumped into the fire with Tiffany. :)<br /><br />Run, rabbit, run!<br /><br /><br /><br />Original Review:<br /><br />Extremely enjoyable witch novel. This sub-branch of the whole disc-World always has the ability to make me giddy and giggly with the feeling that imminent practicality is the most magical skill in the world. It's a consistent feeling, of course, and not endemic to this novel. On the other hand, for all of the Aching novels that all appear to be about coming-to-age, there's a delightful variety in how one can come of age. I got the distinct impression that this 16-year-old woman just got wiser and has received a great deal of Esteem, in a very Maslovian way. It filled my heart to the brim.<br /><br />And by the way, fantastic use of foreshadowing. I'm not even referring to the hare. I caught many of the other ones before they bore fruit (barely) and had to reread the lines I thought I was paying attention to because I kept laughing out of turn when the larger set-up kept kicking me in the shins. So very enjoyable for any novel, but for the Discworlds, I might just put this one at one of the tops.<br /><br />Fantastic stuff. :)</span>

<div id="review_3110988603" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3110988603" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3110988603?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780062067746" href="/book/show/12074855-i-shall-wear-midnight">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3110988603" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6471172528173262857">In this 38th <i>Discworld</i> novel, we once again meet Tiffany Aching. She’s the Chalk’s witch now and supported by the Nac Mac Feegle. However, lately, people have started criticizing her, daring to question what she does. Something unheard of. When the baron dies, Tiffany goes in search of the baron’s son (whom she saved a few books back) so he can take his dead father’s place (he’s away to buy stuff for his soon-to-be-wife). <br/>Thus, she ends up in Ankh-Morpork of all places - and discovers that the s</span>
  <span id="freeText6471172528173262857" style="display:none">In this 38th <i>Discworld</i> novel, we once again meet Tiffany Aching. She’s the Chalk’s witch now and supported by the Nac Mac Feegle. However, lately, people have started criticizing her, daring to question what she does. Something unheard of. When the baron dies, Tiffany goes in search of the baron’s son (whom she saved a few books back) so he can take his dead father’s place (he’s away to buy stuff for his soon-to-be-wife). <br>Thus, she ends up in Ankh-Morpork of all places - and discovers that the situation is even worse there. Apparently, it’s The Cunning Man’s work. No, he’s not really a man, though he used to be. In the meantime, he’s more like an idea whose time has come. Tiffany needs to find a way to defeat The Cunning Man before witches are burnt and things escalate for good (well, bad).<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1595958868i/29867836._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The highlight was seeing Tiffany and the Wee Free Men in the Disc’s most famous … well, notorious … city. The other witches encountered there - not least of which was Escarina Smith, of course - as well as the run-ins with The Watch were just the most mentionable highlights here.<br><br>Many people say that Tiffany’s adventures are weak volumes in the series, that the author’s foray into what many call the YA genre was mistake even. I’m here to tell you it’s all poppycock. <br>The writing was fast-paced and sharp in its observations about human nature, the puns intelligent and fantastic. The dialogues were laugh-out-loud-funny. The animal sidekicks (always a gem in the witches books) were wonderful. The actual topic here, the mystery or threat so to speak, was full of an assortment of mythological tidbits and cultural history, deliciously brought together by the lovely cast of characters.<br><br>So yes, I was once again enchanted. I was angry on behalf of all the hard-working witches and how they had been treated. I was downright homicidal about the nurse, a certain soon-to-be mother-in-law and Amber’s parents (to name but a few). I was delighted by the mischief of our favorite blue-skinned lads. <br>In short: the book, as usual for Terry Pratchett, has snark, meaning, and - most importantly - heart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6471172528173262857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14515982776055069434">I seriously hate the thought of giving any books by Terry Pratchett less than 4 stars, but I had to do it for this one. <br/><br/>I read it twice. My original rating for this book was 3 stars and yep, it stays that way (though at one point I was tempted to downgrade). It was the weakest book of the Tiffany Aching-series .... well until <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25324129.The_Shepherd_s_Crown__Discworld___41_' title='The Shepherd's Crown (Discworld, #41) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Shepherd's Crown</a> was published anyway :( (That one made me infinitely unhappy not just because Sir Terry passed away, but because I found it so bad.)<br/><br/>This was definitely</span>
  <span id="freeText14515982776055069434" style="display:none">I seriously hate the thought of giving any books by Terry Pratchett less than 4 stars, but I had to do it for this one. <br /><br />I read it twice. My original rating for this book was 3 stars and yep, it stays that way (though at one point I was tempted to downgrade). Also I couldn't shake the feeling that Sir Terry was actually spoonfeeding us with the same facts and information basically in every chapter and this put me quite out of countenance with him. <br />Tiffany, whom I always admired for her down-to-earth thinking, first sight and second thoughts, became almost insufferable with her self-anointed martyrdom and basically 50 % of the book was about nothing else but the constant repeat of how much she is doing alone as there is noone else to do it. It became rather tiresome after Chapter 1. <br /><br />As I said, the core story saved the day eventually, but it's far below par the best Discworld standards.</span>

<div id="review_133301565" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133301565" />
    <a title="Lightreads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads"><img alt="Lightreads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1230606449p2/1836077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133301565?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lightreads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lightreads" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads">Lightreads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133301565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2440348449630711095">This is more of the same for this subseries – which is a good thing! More adolescent witch adventures, more growing up too fast, more dry humor with teeth underneath.<br/><br/>Critics go on about how magic in fantasy novels is a metaphor for political power or social power or insert power here. Which is usually a really unsatisfying reading to me because fantasy novel magic is so often inborn, inexplicable, a random or genetic gift. Which <i>is</i> a good metaphor for social power, often, but it’s not very inter</span>
  <span id="freeText2440348449630711095" style="display:none">This is more of the same for this subseries – which is a good thing! More adolescent witch adventures, more growing up too fast, more dry humor with teeth underneath.<br /><br />Critics go on about how magic in fantasy novels is a metaphor for political power or social power or insert power here. Which is usually a really unsatisfying reading to me because fantasy novel magic is so often inborn, inexplicable, a random or genetic gift. Which <i>is</i> a good metaphor for social power, often, but it’s not very interesting – you are powerful because you are, and you are because you are. Okay then.<br /><br />I read this months ago, but I still remember what I liked about it. Tiffany is a really powerful witch, but not in the usual fantasy novel way. Her power is in direct proportion to how smart she is, and how careful. She doesn’t have some inexplicable inner spring of magic, and this book is very clear: Tiffany wasn’t born chosen. She made herself a witch because there was a terrible need; because someone died in a small, common, horrible way; because someone had to do <i>something</i>, and she was there. <i>That</i> is a magic whose metaphors I can get behind. IN fact, the metaphors pretty much collapse. These aren’t books with stupid extended training montages where young people learn magic by chanting a lot. Tiffany learns by living. That’s not a metaphor at all, that’s good literature.<br /><br />I love that. And I love that Tiffany doesn’t always wear power well, that she struggles for compassion, that she feels isolated. I love how almost everyone in this book is faking it inside whatever role they’re living.<br /><br />I don’t love some slightly <i>odd</i> ways this book talks about post-violence trauma, but that’s a whole other conversation.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2440348449630711095" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5107754066221206543">I Shall Wear Midnight: A Story of Discworld (Discworld Novels) 2011, January 1<br/><br/>Oh, goodness, how I loved this book. Wintersmith was okay, but this is fabulous. Tiffany's grown up some more, and now she's home on the chalk, where she's coming into her own as the local witch. Against that, there's someone stirring up witch-hunts again, and Roland has a girlfriend who's the daughter of a Duchess. And the Nac Mac Feegle are into everything, shouting Crivens! Delightful because it gets Tiffany back wh</span>
  <span id="freeText5107754066221206543" style="display:none">I Shall Wear Midnight: A Story of Discworld (Discworld Novels) 2011, January 1<br /><br />Oh, goodness, how I loved this book. Wintersmith was okay, but this is fabulous. Tiffany's grown up some more, and now she's home on the chalk, where she's coming into her own as the local witch. Against that, there's someone stirring up witch-hunts again, and Roland has a girlfriend who's the daughter of a Duchess. And the Nac Mac Feegle are into everything, shouting Crivens! Delightful because it gets Tiffany back where she belongs, and because she gets out a bit, enabling Pratchett to introduce her to all sorts of characters, beloved ones from other books, and new ones. And, everything works out very well indeed, in a most satisfying, and clear sort of way.This will probably be my favorite Pratchett along with The Amazing Maurice and His Educated Rodents, and, of course, Good Omens.<br /><br />Library copy</span>

<div id="review_271851922" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/271851922" />
    <a title="Melissa McShane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1329059-melissa-mcshane"><img alt="Melissa McShane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216147749p2/1329059.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/271851922?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa McShane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa McShane" href="/user/show/1329059-melissa-mcshane">Melissa McShane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=protag-female">protag-female</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer271851922" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4446346471596488997">I realized some ten pages into this book that I'd only ever read it once before, which is unusual for me; I go to Terry Pratchett's books in the times when I can't handle anything else. (This happens often.) It seemed appropriate, given that the US release of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22886868.The_Shepherd_s_Crown__Discworld___41__Tiffany_Aching___5_' title='The Shepherd's Crown (Discworld, #41; Tiffany Aching, #5) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Shepherd's Crown</a> is Tuesday, and turned out to be exactly what I needed on a Sunday evening.<br/><br/>There were things I remembered: Tiffany's changed relationship with Roland, the Nac Mac Feegle in Ankh-Morpork, Letitia the very damp Duchess's </span>
  <span id="freeText4446346471596488997" style="display:none">I realized some ten pages into this book that I'd only ever read it once before, which is unusual for me; I go to Terry Pratchett's books in the times when I can't handle anything else. (This happens often.) It seemed appropriate, given that the US release of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22886868.The_Shepherd_s_Crown__Discworld___41__Tiffany_Aching___5_" title="The Shepherd's Crown (Discworld, #41; Tiffany Aching, #5) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Shepherd's Crown</a> is Tuesday, and turned out to be exactly what I needed on a Sunday evening.<br /><br />There were things I remembered: Tiffany's changed relationship with Roland, the Nac Mac Feegle in Ankh-Morpork, Letitia the very damp Duchess's daughter with unsuspected depth. What I'd forgotten is the strong theme of responsibility that ties the whole thing together. Tiffany Aching, as the new witch of the Chalk, bears responsibility for hundreds of people, most of whom fear her, or possibly fear what she represents. One of Tiffany's gifts is balancing between good and evil, pain and peace, and at the beginning of the book she's become so obsessed with playing that role that she's forgotten, just a little, why it is she's doing it. She's justifiably proud of her skills--she's the girl who kissed the winter, after all--but it's made her somewhat distant from the people she has to serve. So when a formless evil sets its sights on her, she's put in a position where all her skills are, if not useless, at least ineffective to fight her opponent. Woven into the plot is Tiffany's need to grow and understand the world in ways outside her experience. By the end, she knows <i>why</i> witches face tragedy and suffering and the hard choices the way they do, and for all her skills, it's that moment when Tiffany is a true witch.<br /><br />I remember being a little put off by the dissolution of Tiffany and Roland's romance (this is not a spoiler, it's revealed in the first few pages) because that relationship had been telegraphed fairly well in previous books. But the explanation even Tiffany has to accept is that the two of them really were just thrown together, and mistook their both being alone and different for being compatible. Their relationship as future Baron and witch makes a lot more sense, and I like Roland's fiancée Letitia very much, even before we find out her secret. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Letitia as accidental witch amuses me. It fits with young Amber, who seems despite her race to be a born kelda; both girls have abilities no one suspected and both embrace those skills with a sort of careless "well, of course I can do this, what do you mean it's unusual?" that sets up a different type of witch from the ones we've seen to date. Giving a terrifying skeleton a teddy bear to calm him down is brilliant. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />But the heart of any Tiffany Aching story is the Feegles, who were mostly played for laughs in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1086446.Carpe_Jugulum__Discworld___23_" title="Carpe Jugulum (Discworld, #23) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Carpe Jugulum</a> but have steadily become--like pretty much everything about the Discworld--a serious, solid presence in the story. Not that they aren't still hilarious; I love Daft Wullie, who is guaranteed to say the wrong thing every time. But the image of Rob Anybody ready to start a war to defend his wife and family--that's deadly serious. And there are so many serious moments between the funny ones that it's a little strange to look back over the Discworld series and realize those early books were written by the same man.<br /><br />I have no idea what to expect from the final Discworld book. Terry Pratchett's later works, after his illness was serious, aren't quite to the same quality as the earlier ones. What I'm looking for, I think, is a sense of closure. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11275323.Raising_Steam__Discworld___40_" title="Raising Steam (Discworld, #40) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Raising Steam</a>, for all its faults, brought to an end several plot threads that had been unreeling for several books, and I found that satisfying. I look forward to seeing how the final chapter in Tiffany's story plays out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4446346471596488997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124365425" />
    <a title="Nathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/549698-nathan"><img alt="Nathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1192518681p2/549698.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124365425?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan" href="/user/show/549698-nathan">Nathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124365425" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10879698799129310923">I don't find many books that I'd gladly give to my girl and say "this, this is what being human is". In the Tiffany Aching series, Pratchett nailed it and in this, the final book (which can be read alone), Pratchett nails the story too. The others have featured metaphors-come-to-life as antagonists, but they were very active antagonists. In this book, the antagonist is more in the background: he exists, there's pursuit, but it's all playing second fiddle to Tiffany's battle with her feelings and</span>
  <span id="freeText10879698799129310923" style="display:none">I don't find many books that I'd gladly give to my girl and say "this, this is what being human is". In the Tiffany Aching series, Pratchett nailed it and in this, the final book (which can be read alone), Pratchett nails the story too. The others have featured metaphors-come-to-life as antagonists, but they were very active antagonists. In this book, the antagonist is more in the background: he exists, there's pursuit, but it's all playing second fiddle to Tiffany's battle with her feelings and the prejudices of others.<br /><br />There aren't enough stars for this character and this book. You might not like Pratchett's style or his sense of humour but his sense of humanity, and what it means to be human, is unparalleled. He makes us find sympathy for people even as he unveils their ugly side, and writes as though he loves us, warts and all.<br /><br />Loved it to the point of being all teary at the end because of just how fucking perfect it was.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10879698799129310923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_125711825" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125711825" />
    <a title="Carol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1517359-carol"><img alt="Carol" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1221101870p2/1517359.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125711825?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/1517359-carol">Carol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anglophiles, folklore fans, anyone who needs a good laugh, fans of the other Tiffany Aching books</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125711825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13351350139019085659">Terry Pratchett is a genius! This book is the fourth in the Tiffany Aching Adventures, and my favorite so far, I think. Tiffany is a sixteen-year-old witch, self-assured and very wise beyond her years, yet still down to earth (or, in her case, chalk) and still sixteen. She is once again joined by her small, blue, kilted, ale-drinking, fist-fighting, hygienically challenged, oft-invisible clan of Nac Mac Feegles who provide the story's comic relief. Her nemesis this time is the Cunning Man--the p</span>
  <span id="freeText13351350139019085659" style="display:none">Terry Pratchett is a genius! This book is the fourth in the Tiffany Aching Adventures, and my favorite so far, I think. Tiffany is a sixteen-year-old witch, self-assured and very wise beyond her years, yet still down to earth (or, in her case, chalk) and still sixteen. She is once again joined by her small, blue, kilted, ale-drinking, fist-fighting, hygienically challenged, oft-invisible clan of Nac Mac Feegles who provide the story's comic relief. Her nemesis this time is the Cunning Man--the pure, stinking essence of evil left eons ago when a witch-burning fanatic was drawn into the flames himself by one of his young victims. Tiffany realizes that a bit of him exists in all of us as the seed of doubt, fear and xenophobia that can grow into hatred, prejudice and mob violence all too easily. She finds herself facing off against him at a time of change and transition; the old Baron has died peacefully, just a few days before his son, Roland, is to be married. Several of Tiffany's witch sisters arrive for the occasion including Mistress Weatherwax, her severe but ultimately caring mentor, and the bawdy Nanny Ogg (who most willingly fills the bride-to-be in on some wedding night secrets and advice, wink-wink nudge-nudge). As usual, Pratchett had me in alternating tears of hilarity and tenderness and empathy. And, as always, the book ends with the reader feeling a cathartic cleansing, and the sense that Tiffany Aching is in her place, doing her job the best she knows how, and all is right with the world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13351350139019085659" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1442791095" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1442791095" />
    <a title="Carolyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19559507-carolyn"><img alt="Carolyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546411572p2/19559507.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1442791095?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carolyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carolyn" href="/user/show/19559507-carolyn">Carolyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552555593" href="/book/show/9419372-i-shall-wear-midnight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19559507-carolyn?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19559507-carolyn?shelf=humour">humour</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1442791095" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8902127054714689871">It is with some sadness that I finished this knowing that Sir Terry will not write any more Discworld books. He writes in the voice of a 16 year old witch so brilliantly. Although Tiffany Aching is trying very hard to be grown up, she has to keep telling herself she is <i>the</i> witch of the Chalk and the people rely on her to be their witch. But now is no time to be unsure of herself. Something black and evil is hunting her down and putting bad thoughts about witches into people's heads. Tiffany is s</span>
  <span id="freeText8902127054714689871" style="display:none">It is with some sadness that I finished this knowing that Sir Terry will not write any more Discworld books. He writes in the voice of a 16 year old witch so brilliantly. Although Tiffany Aching is trying very hard to be grown up, she has to keep telling herself she is <i>the</i> witch of the Chalk and the people rely on her to be their witch. But now is no time to be unsure of herself. Something black and evil is hunting her down and putting bad thoughts about witches into people's heads. Tiffany is scared but knows the evil will eventually catch up with her and she will have to stand and face it alone. In the meantime she has the Wee Free Men, the Nac Mac Feegle to watch over her (when they're not getting into trouble themselves).<br /><br />An excellent book in this Discworld sub series. Several of our favourite personalities make an appearance,including Granny Weatherwax and Nanny Ogg and the Ankh Morpork Watch. I'm also very glad there is still one more volume to read even if Sir Terry never got to do a final draft and it may still be a little rough in places. It will be sad to say goodbye to all our favourite characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8902127054714689871" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_349494870" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/349494870" />
    <a title="Clouds" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9374686-clouds"><img alt="Clouds" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514641128p2/9374686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/349494870?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clouds" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clouds" href="/user/show/9374686-clouds">Clouds</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=pub-2010s">pub-2010s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=discworld">discworld</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer349494870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4763789162977379816"><a href='http://www.theaceofclouds.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380317319i/689370.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><b>Following the resounding success of my <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=2' rel='nofollow noopener'>Locus Quest</a>, I faced a dilemma: which reading list to follow it up with? Variety is the spice of life, so I’ve decided to diversify and pursue six different lists simultaneously. This book falls into my <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=4' rel='nofollow noopener'>LOCUS Y-A</a> list.<br/><br/>I think I’ll always have a soft-spot for imaginative young-adult speculative fiction and as the good people at Locus did such a grand job with picking their Sci-Fi winners, I’ll trust them to single out some special y-a books too.</b><br/><br/>I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7576115-i-shall-wear-midnight-discworld-38' rel='nofollow noopener'>
 </a></span>
  <span id="freeText4763789162977379816" style="display:none"><a href="http://www.theaceofclouds.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380317319i/689370.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Following the resounding success of my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=2" rel="nofollow noopener">Locus Quest</a>, I faced a dilemma: which reading list to follow it up with? Variety is the spice of life, so I’ve decided to diversify and pursue six different lists simultaneously. This book falls into my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=4" rel="nofollow noopener">LOCUS Y-A</a> list.<br><br>I think I’ll always have a soft-spot for imaginative young-adult speculative fiction and as the good people at Locus did such a grand job with picking their Sci-Fi winners, I’ll trust them to single out some special y-a books too.</b><br><br>I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7576115-i-shall-wear-midnight-discworld-38" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>
    <b>I Shall Wear Midnight</b>
  </i>
</a> a couple of months ago, and put-off writing my review until I could untangle my mixed reaction.<br><br>(- <b><i>**</i>NOTE:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7576115-i-shall-wear-midnight-discworld-38" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>
    <b>I Shall Wear Midnight</b>
  </i>
</a> is not actually a Locus Y-A Award winner - but the first three books in this sequence were, so I included this in my award reading list because I'm a completionist. -)<br><br>I first came to the Tiffany Aching sequence sceptically. To my mind, there was no need (apart from marketing considerations) to deliberately create a new Discworld character targeting the Young-Adult niche. I started reading the Discworld books when I was twelve, and still love them now, just shy of thirty. Pratchett has always straddled the line between fantasy and satire with incredible ease, and that blend offers something for readers of all ages. So I was cynical of this YA series; it sounded more like a publisher's idea, not Terry's.<br><br>But give the man scraps and he will cook you something sublime (at least, most the time). Pratchett's little heroine won me over big-time. She's awesome - really, an excellent job. I could gush for some time, but let me stay on point. I came to the sequence sceptical, and the first three books made a covert of me, which is why, by the time I reached <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7576115-i-shall-wear-midnight-discworld-38" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>
    <b>I Shall Wear Midnight</b>
  </i>
</a> (book four following Tiffany) I was comfortably expecting something special - I'd pulled a U'ey. Thus; dissapointment.<br><br>I thought it fell a bit flat. Well, a lot flat. Tiff has grown-up a great deal by this point and all the relationship dynamics are skewed. A big element of the story involves Tiff haring (no pun intended) off to Ankh-Morpork and I thought "aw yeah, this is going to be great!" - but you know what, it's not. The city... does not sing. There are a few cameo appearances for the Watch, and there's a new city-witch introduced (who didn't do much for me) - but by and large the city itself was very generic... it just didn't really feel all that Ankh-Morporkey to me. Big opportunity lost to crash a great character into a great setting. Anticlimatic.<br><br>Throughout the book there are little 'omen' moments, forsehadowing Tiffany's eureka-moment when she saves the day (that shouldn't count as a spoiler to anyone, right?) but as familiar as I am with Sir Terry's plot-weaving skills, these felt surprisingly unsubtle, ham-fisted and poorly integrated. <br><br>The nemesis Tiffany faces in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7576115-i-shall-wear-midnight-discworld-38" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>
    <b>I Shall Wear Midnight</b>
  </i>
</a> played too many cards similar to the Hiver monster she defeated in a previous book, and despite all efforts to play the big-bad-ominous card, Tiffany has grown into an exceptionally capable young woman and never, ever, seemed seriously at risk.<br><br>I can scan a shelf of Discworld books and tell you my favourite scene from each and every one - except for <i>Jingo</i>, <i>Pyramids</i>, <i>Lords &amp; Ladies</i>... and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7576115-i-shall-wear-midnight-discworld-38" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>
    <b>I Shall Wear Midnight</b>
  </i>
</a>. Sorry, Terry - for me, this one was a dud.<br><br>But I still gave it three stars because despite all these flaws I still 'liked' it - I never thought, "ye, gawds, must I go back to that tome of trash again?!". I was always happy to snatch it up for my lunch break - (and let's be honest, I would happily read <i>An In-Depth Guide to Precision Grouting</i> if it was written by Pratchett) - but my attention drifted frequently and by the end I was keen to move onto the next book on my list (which was better).<br><br><b>After this I read: </b><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/366652166" rel="nofollow noopener">Leviathan</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4763789162977379816" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_132273230" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/132273230" />
    <a title="Rachel Hartman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4545538-rachel-hartman"><img alt="Rachel Hartman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442465555p2/4545538.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/132273230?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel Hartman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel Hartman" href="/user/show/4545538-rachel-hartman">Rachel Hartman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer132273230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14424715414451945394">Bah. Made me cry. Curse you Terry Pratchett! <br/><br/><i>Hat Made of Sky</i> is still my favourite of the Tiffany Aching books, but I might even put this one second. The plot wasn't as tight as he (historically) has been capable of, and the menace of the villain didn't live up to its potential, quite. But there was so much other stuff here to love, and be moved by, and remind me of other things, that I didn't really care.<br/><br/>For the first half, seriously, I could only read about forty pages at a time because then I</span>
  <span id="freeText14424715414451945394" style="display:none">Bah. Made me cry. Curse you Terry Pratchett! <br /><br /><i>Hat Made of Sky</i> is still my favourite of the Tiffany Aching books, but I might even put this one second. The plot wasn't as tight as he (historically) has been capable of, and the menace of the villain didn't live up to its potential, quite. But there was so much other stuff here to love, and be moved by, and remind me of other things, that I didn't really care.<br /><br />For the first half, seriously, I could only read about forty pages at a time because then I would get sad and have to go off by myself and think about things. I know people complain that the Tiffany Aching books are a bit didactic, or a bit obvious, but I think it's more complicated than that. It's like a beloved grandparent sitting you down and telling you his hard-earned wisdom about what it means to be human, and what's good in life. And, depending where you are in your own life, it may all seem rather trivial and obvious (because you're young and you already know everything, duh), or it may seem the old man is telling you the only knowledge worth knowing (because you've been through those fires, you have fought and lost, and earned this selfsame wisdom for your pains).<br /><br />There's an urgency to these latter books, as if he has looked his own mortality in the eye and said, "Hold on, Death, there's a few last things I want to get off my chest." Maybe it's not as elegantly done as when he was younger, but he still just makes my jaw drop with the things he understands, and the depth to which he understands them. He is a thinker after my own heart.<br /><br />I sometimes feel my reading superpower consists in being able to read between the lines, to see the writer behind the writing. I saw Terry Pratchett behind all the witticisms before he started tipping his hand more deliberately. In this particular book, he is transparent in every sentence; he's not even trying to fade into the background. It's sad, and it's lovely, and it felt like one long goodbye. I cried at the end -- and he actually did manage to surprise me, the old fox, which made me cry the more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14424715414451945394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_128159782" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/128159782" />
    <a title="Jen3n" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1213541-jen3n"><img alt="Jen3n" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1212627901p2/1213541.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/128159782?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen3n" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen3n" href="/user/show/1213541-jen3n">Jen3n</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213541-jen3n?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213541-jen3n?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer128159782" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5022057865780418313">I still feel weird putting these under the heading of "children’s books." They aren't, really, and never have been. Even when the main character was just a ten year old girl.<br/><br/>So. This is the last of the Tiffany Aching books and the last time we will ever hear of Granny Weatherwax or Nanny Ogg ever again, thanks to Mr. Pratchett's disease. I am given to understand that most of this book was, by necessity, dictated.<br/><br/>And it's not a bad book. Quite good, given the circumstances. But a lot of character</span>
  <span id="freeText5022057865780418313" style="display:none">I still feel weird putting these under the heading of "children’s books." They aren't, really, and never have been. Even when the main character was just a ten year old girl.<br /><br />So. This is the last of the Tiffany Aching books and the last time we will ever hear of Granny Weatherwax or Nanny Ogg ever again, thanks to Mr. Pratchett's disease. I am given to understand that most of this book was, by necessity, dictated.<br /><br />And it's not a bad book. Quite good, given the circumstances. But a lot of characters from the Discworld seemed to just show up out of no where. It was like they were saying goodbye. Parts of it felt, when I read them, the way that last scene from the movie Labyrinth felt when I watched it. You know, when Hoggle and Ludo and everyone are telling Sarah goodbye. "If you should need us ..."<br /><br />Man. I'm sort of tearing up. Hold on a sec.<br /><br />AND we finally get to find out what the hell happened to Esk. I've been wondering about her for years. No lie. It was nice. It felt tidy and correct.<br /><br />Parts of this book were really rather rough. It was much darker in places than many of his other books. I mean, any book that starts out with the near-death of a thirteen year old pregnant girl because her father tried to beat her into the ground is going down a rather particular path, if you know what I mean.<br /><br />Other parts felt a little rushed. Like the love-interest. I'm not spoiling anything to say there is one in this book. Nor am I spoiling anything to say that it felt very sudden.<br /><br />However, over all, this is an excellent end to the Aching series and a rather good book from my favorite author of Modern fantasy.<br /><br />Highly recommended.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5022057865780418313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13693918010817055779"><i>I Shall Wear Midnight</i> is the fourth book in the <i>Tiffany Aching</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. <br/><br/>In this story, there is an entity that is causing an increase of bigotry against witches, and naturally its prime target is Tiffany. The story was ok, but very short and the plot was pretty thin. The book was carried by the characters, who are all still a lot of fun. I liked that we got to spend a short amount of time in Ankh-Morpork, which isn’t a normal setting for the Tiffany books, and I especially liked t</span>
  <span id="freeText13693918010817055779" style="display:none"><i>I Shall Wear Midnight</i> is the fourth book in the <i>Tiffany Aching</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. <br /><br />In this story, there is an entity that is causing an increase of bigotry against witches, and naturally its prime target is Tiffany. The story was ok, but very short and the plot was pretty thin. The book was carried by the characters, who are all still a lot of fun. I liked that we got to spend a short amount of time in Ankh-Morpork, which isn’t a normal setting for the Tiffany books, and I especially liked that we <i>finally</i> got a tie back to the first Witches book, <i>Equal Rites</i>. The timeline is rather questionable, but I was just happy to see some reference to it.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_115951846" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115951846" />
    <a title="Nicole" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/163750-nicole"><img alt="Nicole" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1189653481p2/163750.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115951846?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole" href="/user/show/163750-nicole">Nicole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780061433061" href="/book/show/10432128-i-shall-wear-midnight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/163750-nicole?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/163750-nicole?shelf=favourite-characters">favourite-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/163750-nicole?shelf=fierce-females">fierce-females</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115951846" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8347212803327417572">4 1/2 stars. My objection to teenage marriage, even in a pseudo-18th century fantasyland, prevents me from finding this book perfect. But it's close.<br/>I stalled a bit in getting to this one because it's the last* of the Tiffany Aching books, and I hate to see a good thing end (even though I can re-read the books at my leisure). Tiffany, as I've said in previous reviews, is a wonderful character. She's a far better person than I am, but somehow I relate to her--in part because she continually ends </span>
  <span id="freeText8347212803327417572" style="display:none">4 1/2 stars. My objection to teenage marriage, even in a pseudo-18th century fantasyland, prevents me from finding this book perfect. But it's close.<br />I stalled a bit in getting to this one because it's the last* of the Tiffany Aching books, and I hate to see a good thing end (even though I can re-read the books at my leisure). Tiffany, as I've said in previous reviews, is a wonderful character. She's a far better person than I am, but somehow I relate to her--in part because she continually ends up with the fuzzy end of the lollipop. But she always rises to the challenge. She's book-smart and clever, she's resourceful, she's generally kind even when she doesn't feel it inside. She's fantastically human and wonderfully witchy. She takes on all the less glamourous aspects of being a witch (being doctor, nurse, midwife, counselor, and mortician, among other things) in stride; and sometimes she gets to do a fancy bit of magic. <br />Anyone who's read and loved the Harry Potter books needs to read the Tiffany Aching books. She could stand toe-to-toe with Hermione Granger and never blink...and she'd be her friend, too. <br />Pratchett has a true gift for portraying three-dimensional female characters. He doesn't stoop to easy stereotypes and catty female rivalry. In this book in particular, I liked how Tiffany and Letitia come to understand each other and how Tiffany and Mrs. Proust deal with the Duchess. I enjoyed the appearances by other witches from previous books, as well.<br />The Roland situation made me sad at first, but I was made to see that things do work out for the best. Preston is delightful, with his sometimes cleverly concealed cleverness, his curious and word-loving mind, and his loyalty. The Nac Mac Feegle are hilarious as ever, Tiffany's brave and devious allies. The Cunning Man and his mind-poisoning are Tiffany's toughest challenge yet, also calling to mind real-life history.<br />As with <i>Wintersmith</i>, I found myself shedding a few tears when I reached the end of the story, not because it's sad, but because it's beautiful and has such a ring of truth and a life-affirming quality to it--sort of like crying at a graduation or wedding. Although I also wish there could be more Tiffany stories.*<br />If you haven't read this series, please try it (starting with <i>The Wee Free Men</i>). Disregard the "YA" label; these books should be on the shelf next to the so-called adult Pratchett books. If you have read them, I hope you agree with me that Terry Pratchett is a literary treasure and a humanist of the first order.<br /><br />* When I read this, this was the last of the Tiffany books. Some time later, <i>The Shepherd's Crown</i> was released.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8347212803327417572" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_124664520" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124664520" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2153392-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1242479070p2/2153392.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124664520?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/2153392-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2153392-nate?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2153392-nate?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124664520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16960784183451890710">Sometimes I feel like there's not enough joy left in the world. Then Sir Pratchett publishes another gem like this, and it makes me feel better.<br/><br/>Tiffany Aching is 16. In a world that looks a lot like our 19th century, that's taking the 'young' out of Young Adult. This is a book with genuine terror, truly adult situations, and more joy and laughter than you'll find almost anywhere. <br/><br/>Tiffany is now the true witch of her Chalk; she's bandaging wounds, helping the old and sick shuffle off their mortal</span>
  <span id="freeText16960784183451890710" style="display:none">Sometimes I feel like there's not enough joy left in the world. Then Sir Pratchett publishes another gem like this, and it makes me feel better.<br /><br />Tiffany Aching is 16. In a world that looks a lot like our 19th century, that's taking the 'young' out of Young Adult. This is a book with genuine terror, truly adult situations, and more joy and laughter than you'll find almost anywhere. <br /><br />Tiffany is now the true witch of her Chalk; she's bandaging wounds, helping the old and sick shuffle off their mortal coil, and dealing with viciously abusive parents (not her own). And now, she's woken something truly evil, something that wants to see every witch stoned or burned at the stake. And because she's the kind of person who takes responsibility seriously, she knows she has to deal with it herself. Fortunately for her the Nac Mac Feegle take few things seriously, and that includes their promises not to help. We get to see a number of fan favorites; the City Watch makes an appearance, the first female wizard sticks her head up again, and Wee Mad Arthur discovers his roots. <br /><br />The book started a bit slow--or if not slow, like some of the language wasn't quite doing the story justice. But then it rockets along, and the true masterpiece it is starts to shine through.<br /><br />If you've got an 8 or 10 yr old who loves the Tiffany Aching stories, I'd wait a couple years before introducing them to this one; teen pregnancy, attempted suicides, murderers, and a truly terrifying villain without redeeming qualities might be too much for them to handle. But you owe it to yourself to read this book, and see how Tiffany's story comes to an end. It's almost perfect. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16960784183451890710" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
