<div id="review_789268820" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/789268820" />
    <a title="Martyn Stanley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10866625-martyn-stanley"><img alt="Martyn Stanley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446815001p2/10866625.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/789268820?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martyn Stanley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martyn Stanley" href="/user/show/10866625-martyn-stanley">Martyn Stanley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer789268820" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5710297751631120066">I wrote this review ages ago, thought I'd share it here also!<br/><br/>It's quite ironic that I last reviewed Equal Rites, a milestone book in the series which focused on character development more than narrative and farce as the first two books. It also introduced the iconic 'Granny Weatherwax' who is something of the archetypal witch.<br/><br/>The irony is most significant in the fact that Equal Rites was all about a character called 'Eskarina Smith' a little girl who ends up becoming a wizard and eventually a wi</span>
  <span id="freeText5710297751631120066" style="display:none">I wrote this review ages ago, thought I'd share it here also!<br><br>It's quite ironic that I last reviewed Equal Rites, a milestone book in the series which focused on character development more than narrative and farce as the first two books. It also introduced the iconic 'Granny Weatherwax' who is something of the archetypal witch.<br><br>The irony is most significant in the fact that Equal Rites was all about a character called 'Eskarina Smith' a little girl who ends up becoming a wizard and eventually a witch. After Equal Rites we never heard of her again... Until now.<br><br>It's interesting that the history of another minor Discworld character 'Wee Mad Arthur' of the city watch is also looked at in more detail - yet this is a book which the author doesn't seem to want us to consider part of the main series?<br><br>I say that because as with all of the Tiffany Aching books, we are warned that is for younger readers. Now this I found appropriate in the Wee Free Men, and to a degree the other Tiffany Books too - but I find it sits less comfortable in 'I Shall Wear Midnight' It's a book about growing up - like the other 'Aching' books, but it focuses on morality and character development to a greater degree and it covers some fairly adult themes. Personally I don't think it's as suitable for younger readers as the other 'Aching' books. That said I really enjoyed it, found it very hard to put down and read it in three days.<br><br>At the start of 'I Shall Wear Midnight' Tiffany is a fully fledged witch going about her business, but there are strange forces at work people are becoming fearful of witches and starting to persecute them. <br><br>An unfortunate scene where a dying Baron is witnessed with the Witch results in further mistrust and fear and Tiffany has to take a trip to Ankh Morpork - accompanied of course by the Nac Mac Feegles. It turns out the Feegles are nearly almost as spectacular at rebuilding things as they are at destroying them.... Tiffany meets the infamous owner of 'Boffo's Emporium' and the mysterious Eskarina Smith... <br><br>The whole plot slowly turns into a who dunnit, the it being the summoning of an ancient power known as 'The Cunning Man' who was an old Omnian witch finder. There's a wedding taking place at the castle, the older witches turn up, but the Cunning Man is close behind.<br><br>This is a book full of folklore references and surprises and it's hard to say alot about it without spoiling some of the fun.<br><br>All in all it's a fantastic book, possibly the best in the 'Tiffany Aching / Nac Mac Feegle' series although I would have liked more confrontations with the Cunning Man, and I thought it was a little too adult themed to still bear the subtitle 'aimed at younger readers'.<br><br>Martyn Stanley<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23154350.The_Last_Dragon_Slayer__Deathsworn_Arc___1_" title="The Last Dragon Slayer (Deathsworn Arc, #1) by Martyn Stanley" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1412022977l/23154350._SY75_.jpg" alt="The Last Dragon Slayer (Deathsworn Arc, #1) by Martyn Stanley" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23245836.The_Verkreath_Horror__Deathsworn_Arc___2_" title="The Verkreath Horror (Deathsworn Arc, #2) by Martyn Stanley" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1411765112l/23245836._SY75_.jpg" alt="The Verkreath Horror (Deathsworn Arc, #2) by Martyn Stanley" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23294385.The_Blood_Queen__Deathsworn_Arc___3_" title="The Blood Queen (Deathsworn Arc, #3) by Martyn Stanley" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1412792938l/23294385._SY75_.jpg" alt="The Blood Queen (Deathsworn Arc, #3) by Martyn Stanley" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22351431.The_Blood_Queen__Deathsworn_Arc___3_" title="The Blood Queen (Deathsworn Arc, #3) by Martyn Stanley" rel="nofollow noopener">

</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25417749.The_Lambton_Worm" title="The Lambton Worm by Martyn Stanley" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1429864692l/25417749._SX50_.jpg" alt="The Lambton Worm by Martyn Stanley" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>Deathsworn Arc: The Last Dragon Slayer = FREE!<br><br>*Edit: Finished re-reading to my kids 01/07/2020. I stand by everything I said in the original review. This is a great book. An intriguing story and the Cunning Man is a suitable adversary for Tiffany (Who is fast becoming a powerful witch). There was noticeably less Nac Mac Feegle through chunks of this book. They faded into the background a bit and I think that's sad. However, it WAS nice to catch up with Eskarina again and Mrs Proust was a great addition to the cast. I thoroughly enjoyed the re-read and my kids enjoyed having it read to the for the first time.<br><br>If you're looking for a new fantasy series, why not dip your toes into the world of Deathsworn Arc? The Prequel novella '<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/42526008.Lady_Death__Deathsworn_Arc___0_5_" title="Lady Death (Deathsworn Arc, #0.5) by Martyn Stanley" rel="nofollow noopener">Lady Death</a> is currently FREE on Amazon and other ebook retailers:- <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://amzn.to/38l8L0h">https://amzn.to/38l8L0h</a></span>

<div id="review_123201962" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/123201962" />
    <a title="Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1605591-christine"><img alt="Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252166169p2/1605591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/123201962?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christine" href="/user/show/1605591-christine">Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780061433047" href="/book/show/7739862-i-shall-wear-midnight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=fantasy-humor">fantasy-humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=feminist-fiction">feminist-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=fantasy-myth-and-fairy">fantasy-myth-and-fairy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=witches-and-wizards">witches-and-wizards</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer123201962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7190486839718526797"><i>I Shall Wear Midnight</i> is supposedly that last novel about the young witch from the Chalk, Tiffany. In some ways that knowledge colors the book.<br/><br/>Tiffany has done with her education and is back home serving as the Chalk's witch. Sadly, strange things seem to be happening, more than just what happens with an senient cheese named Horace, a lawyer who is frog (but who can be paid in beetles) and the Feegles around.<br/><br/>In some ways, the novel feels like a good-bye, if not to the Disc than to Tiffany. This </span>
  <span id="freeText7190486839718526797" style="display:none"><i>I Shall Wear Midnight</i> is supposedly that last novel about the young witch from the Chalk, Tiffany. In some ways that knowledge colors the book.<br /><br />Tiffany has done with her education and is back home serving as the Chalk's witch. Sadly, strange things seem to be happening, more than just what happens with an senient cheese named Horace, a lawyer who is frog (but who can be paid in beetles) and the Feegles around.<br /><br />In some ways, the novel feels like a good-bye, if not to the Disc than to Tiffany. This makes the novel a little bittersweet in tone. It also isn't quite as tight as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2855034.Nation" title="Nation by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Nation</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6250169.Unseen_Academicals__Discworld___37_" title="Unseen Academicals (Discworld, #37) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Unseen Academicals</a>. It also feels like Pratchett is showing writers like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1077326.J_K__Rowling" title="J.K. Rowling" rel="nofollow noopener">J.K. Rowling</a> and others that fantasy can be more than just magic. The story takes left turns out points that can be shocking or, to be more extact, might make parents uncomfortable. Yet, considering Pratchett's sheer knowledge about folklore and folktales, it could be said that he is returning fairy tales and fairy stories to thier original purpose.<br /><br />And there does seem to be something deeper in the book than the simple plot. If <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2855034.Nation" title="Nation by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Nation</a> was about belief and community, then this book too is about community, but also about acceptance. Tiffany's battle in the book isn't just acceptance of others as her as the witch, but also her need to accept what others are.<br /><br />For the long time Disc fan, several loved characters make appearances, including various witches and members of the Watch.<br /><br />The book is a fitting conclusion to the Tiffany sub-series. It has been a pleasure watching her grow up.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7190486839718526797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_185680920" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185680920" />
    <a title="Lee Battersby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1101417-lee-battersby"><img alt="Lee Battersby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471081642p2/1101417.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185680920?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Battersby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Battersby" href="/user/show/1101417-lee-battersby">Lee Battersby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185680920" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7731620360664759125">Pratchett-by-numbers, with nothing memorable or engaging to tie the plot around. Characters and subplots are added for no good reason and resolved without the slightest sign of conflict or character change, the antagonist is poorly defined and, in the end, despatched with such ease that it leaves the reader wondering just what the fuss was all about-- if this thing has been the scourge of witches throughout the centuries then the witches can't have been very good at what they do to have been bea</span>
  <span id="freeText7731620360664759125" style="display:none">Pratchett-by-numbers, with nothing memorable or engaging to tie the plot around. Characters and subplots are added for no good reason and resolved without the slightest sign of conflict or character change, the antagonist is poorly defined and, in the end, despatched with such ease that it leaves the reader wondering just what the fuss was all about-- if this thing has been the scourge of witches throughout the centuries then the witches can't have been very good at what they do to have been beaten by such a lame-ass baddie. The usual sharp Pratchett wit is buried under long, rambling asides that (normally so witty and insightful) just resemble a storyteller getting sidetracked without the discipline to keep on track. And, ultimately, it feels like sound and fury signifying less than nothing.<br /><br /> All in all this is just a very muddy, loose, unfocussed work, where even the tricks Pratchett normally uses to keep the reader entertained whilst he wanders away from the plot fail to work. Easily one of the worst Discworld novels to date. Deeply disappointing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7731620360664759125" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_127828726" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/127828726" />
    <a title="Peterb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1112441-peterb"><img alt="Peterb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1421680881p2/1112441.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/127828726?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peterb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peterb" href="/user/show/1112441-peterb">Peterb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer127828726" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12056965140080024817">I Shall Wear Midnight is Terry Pratchett's final book in the Tiffany Aching series. While clever, thoughtful, and well-constructed, it suffers from the same problem Pratchett has had in his other recent books: he has fallen too much in love with his characters to truly hurt them. Compared to the latent menace that suffused, for example, The Wee Free Men, we never feel here that Tiffany is at any risk that she can't overcome through prodigious application of witch-bourne moxie. This is a drawback</span>
  <span id="freeText12056965140080024817" style="display:none">I Shall Wear Midnight is Terry Pratchett's final book in the Tiffany Aching series. While clever, thoughtful, and well-constructed, it suffers from the same problem Pratchett has had in his other recent books: he has fallen too much in love with his characters to truly hurt them. Compared to the latent menace that suffused, for example, The Wee Free Men, we never feel here that Tiffany is at any risk that she can't overcome through prodigious application of witch-bourne moxie. This is a drawback. A number of the other Discworld characters appear, briefly in the book; even antihero-turned-completely-boring Sam Vimes (am I the only person who is tired of him yet?) makes an appearance.<br /><br />I don't mean to sound ungrateful. I realize this is a book for young adults, and so it isn't appropriate to expect the literary equivalent of Werner Herzog. But compared to 2008's superb Nation, also a book for young adults, which was infused with a sense of legitimate dread and danger from the first page, this novel feels more like a cop-out. Where Nation was a gallop on a wild horse, I Shall Wear Midnight was more like a well-constructed carousel: attractive, even pleasant, but not thrilling.<br /><br />I enjoyed I Shall Wear Midnight. I would even recommend it. But that missing fifth star is for what might have been, and isn't.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12056965140080024817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review127828726">
  <td colspan="2">
    <div class="updateActionLinks">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3575401447" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3575401447" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5129776-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1331301021p2/5129776.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3575401447?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/5129776-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552555593" href="/book/show/9419372-i-shall-wear-midnight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5129776-chris?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5129776-chris?shelf=discworld">discworld</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3575401447" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13032210539727757419"><blockquote>How now, you secret, black, and midnight hags? What is't you do?<br/>-- <strong>Macbeth</strong> IV/1</blockquote><br/>Terry Pratchett is full of surprises. Because this, the fourth in the Tiffany Aching series of Discworld novels, is marketed as 'for younger readers' one might not anticipate that this is considerably darker than its predecessors, despite the expected humour and wit. And yet, with Tiffany being fifteen going on sixteen, perhaps with her growing maturity a more realistic view of what's possible in Discworld is inevitab</span>
  <span id="freeText13032210539727757419" style="display:none"><blockquote>How now, you secret, black, and midnight hags? What is't you do?<br />-- <strong>Macbeth</strong> IV/1</blockquote><br />Terry Pratchett is full of surprises. Because this, the fourth in the Tiffany Aching series of Discworld novels, is marketed as 'for younger readers' one might not anticipate that this is considerably darker than its predecessors, despite the expected humour and wit. And yet, with Tiffany being fifteen going on sixteen, perhaps with her growing maturity a more realistic view of what's possible in Discworld is inevitable.<br /><br />Neil Gaiman, in his introduction to Pratchett's collection <em>
  <a href="https://wp.me/s2oNj1-slip" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">A Slip of the Keyboard</a>
</em>, noted that "There is a fury to Terry Pratchett’s writing," and that is more than evident here in the stark opening and much of what follows. Some of that rage may have been tied up with his diagnosis for Alzheimer's a couple of years before, but he had always been furious about injustices and that comes through very strongly here.<br /><br />But don't think <em>I Shall Wear Midnight</em> is a miserable instalment in Tiffany's story: this is a heart-warming coming-of-age tale, even for a young witch who's already mature and responsible beyond her years. The interweaving of the traditional folksong <em>Pleasant and Delightful</em> gives -- for old folkies like me, born the same year as Pratchett -- the story an added piquancy with its themes of love, leave-taking and loss, and may bring a tear or two to the eye.<br /><br /><blockquote>It was pleasant and delightful on a bright summer's morn,<br />When the fields and the meadows were covered in corn,<br />There were blackbirds and thrushes singing on every tree,<br />And the larks they sang melodious at the dawning of the day...</blockquote><br />Being a witch on the Chalk has meant that Tiffany has become a combination of district nurse, midwife and unstinting care worker for her community, albeit one who can work magic. And she does it diligently because she <em>does</em> care about her community. But in recent months she has noticed a wariness amongst some people that occasionally manifests as suspicions and mutterings. And then there is an unprovoked fatal attack on a solitary old woman; and she witnesses the antagonism of a crowd who collect when a father beats his pregnant daughter so badly she has a miscarriage.<br /><br />Furthermore there are the ramifications that arise when she removes the abused daughter to the safety of the mound of the Nac Mac Feegles, and the accusations of theft from and the murder of the Baron, which then sees her arraigned by her former friend Roland, the Baron's son and heir, who is due to be married. Can it really all be because of something she herself was responsible for?<br /><br />Tiffany has had mountains of monstrous challenges every year or two over the course of four novels -- antagonists have included the Fairy Queen, a being called a hiver, and the Wintersmith -- and now it's another opponent, one who's poisoning minds against witches. Pratchett had previously featured a witchfinder in <em>Good Omens</em>, his collaboration with Neil Gaiman, and another now appears in the person of the Cunning Man. Pratchett knows his folklore but has further fashioned this figure as the antithesis of Tiffany for reasons the novel hints at, the Cunning Man's motivation being to scapegoat -- with as much vehemence and malevolence as he can muster -- the community witch.<br /><br />There is, as ever, so much to admire and enjoy here: the Feegles and their loyalty to their Kelda, to their bigjob "hag o' the hills" and to the fey Amber; Tiffany's often mutating relationships with the Baron, with Roland and his bride-to-be -- the surprising Letitia -- with her own father, and of course with her older colleagues, Granny Weatherwax <em>et al</em>; I mustn't forget Mrs Proust the purveyor of boffo merchandise in Ankh-Morpork (whose name, by the way, is an anagram of the surname of Professor Sprout of Hogwarts), nor the novice man-at-arms Preston who, I anticipate, may well reappear in the final Tiffany novel.<br /><br />And I was glad to be reacquainted with Eskarina Smith, the admirable female wizard who featured in <em>
  <a href="https://wp.me/s2oNj1-rites" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Equal Rites</a>
</em> and whom I feared we'd heard the last of. But the spotlight must rest on Miss Aching herself, whose transformation from apprentice witch in sky-blue dress to midnight hag in black is accomplished through a baptism of fire in which she encounters death, abuse and antagonism and yet is <em>still</em> able to forgive, if not forget.<br /><br />And ever and anon the lines of the folksong remind us of the fragility of our lives.<br /><blockquote>And the ship she lies waiting for the fast flowing tide,<br />And if ever I return again,<br />I will make you my bride.</blockquote><br /><br />________<br /><br /><p>
  The previous Tiffany Aching books (<em>links are to my reviews</em>) were
</p><br /> <br />	 <em><a href="https://wp.me/s2oNj1-weefree" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wee Free Men</a></em> (2003): 9 years old; <br />	 <em><a href="https://wp.me/s2oNj1-hat" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">A Hat Full of Sky</a></em> (2004): 11 years old; <br />	 <em><a href="https://wp.me/s2oNj1-smith" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Wintersmith</a></em> (2006): 13 years old. <br /> <br /><p>
  The fifth in the series (and the last of the Discworld novels) is <em>The Shepherd's Crown</em>.
</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="13032210539727757419" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VFxIUzvqac0vRExj9/LoQs01Gnv9vjbNmzeqV6k5qt1GtIK0GQQHMkkmWi8/5H9pM0VlpY30wHa7TxgnKLQ5ZA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5Nk4DjHZhnTixiwdgh6AsDwtkCqkgXGe93CLaWBmdPr2MfLpEzfoi4SkOlFKCBebwl3v9NTLhyXXCDkZ4evnQw==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett">About Terry Pratchett</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16711698416039388732">Born Terence David John Pratchett, Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <i>The Carpet People</i>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br/><br/>Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, i</span>
  <span id="freeText16711698416039388732" style="display:none">Born Terence David John Pratchett, Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <i>The Carpet People</i>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br /><br />Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, including his first Discworld novel, <i>The Color of Magic</i>, in 1983. In 1987, he turned to writing full time. <br /><br />There are over 40 books in the Discworld series, of which four are written for children. The first of these, <i>The Amazing Maurice and His Educated Rodents</i>, won the Carnegie Medal. <br /><br />A non-Discworld book, <i>Good Omens</i>, his 1990 collaboration with Neil Gaiman, has been a longtime bestseller and was reissued in hardcover by William Morrow in early 2006 (it is also available as a mass market paperback - Harper Torch, 2006 - and trade paperback - Harper Paperbacks, 2006). <br /><br />In 2008, Harper Children's published Terry's standalone non-Discworld YA novel, <i>Nation</i>. Terry published <i>Snuff</i> in October 2011. <br /><br />Regarded as one of the most significant contemporary English-language satirists, Pratchett has won numerous literary awards, was named an Officer of the British Empire (OBE) “for services to literature” in 1998, and has received honorary doctorates from the University of Warwick in 1999, the University of Portsmouth in 2001, the University of Bath in 2003, the University of Bristol in 2004, Buckinghamshire New University in 2008, the University of Dublin in 2008, Bradford University in 2009, the University of Winchester in 2009, and The Open University in 2013 for his contribution to Public Service.<br /><br />In Dec. of 2007, Pratchett disclosed that he had been diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease. On 18 Feb, 2009, he was knighted by Queen Elizabeth II.<br /><br />He was awarded the World Fantasy Life Achievement Award in 2010.<br /><br />Sir Terry Pratchett passed away on 12th March 2015.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16711698416039388732" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
